    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41598"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome of Tanacetum cinerariifolium , the natural source of mosquito coil"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Reports 2019 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-12-03"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Pyrethrum (Tanacetum cinerariifolium), which is a perennial Asteraceae plant with white daisy-like flowers, is the original source of mosquito coils and is known for the biosynthesis of the pyrethrin class of natural insecticides. However, the molecular basis of the production of pyrethrins by T. cinerariifolium has yet to be fully elucidated. Here, we present the 7.1-Gb draft genome of T. cinerariifolium, consisting of 2,016,451 scaffolds and 60,080 genes predicted with high confidence. Notably, analyses of transposable elements (TEs) indicated that TEs occupy 33.84% of the genome sequence. Furthermore, TEs of the sire and oryco clades were found to be enriched in the T. cinerariifolium-specific evolutionary lineage, occupying a total of 13% of the genome sequence, a proportion approximately 8-fold higher than that in other plants. InterProScan analysis demonstrated that biodefense-related toxic proteins (e.g., ribosome inactivating proteins), signal transduction-related proteins (e.g., histidine kinases), and metabolic enzymes (e.g., lipoxygenases, acyl-CoA dehydrogenases/oxygenases, and P450s) are also highly enriched in the T. cinerariifolium genome. Molecular phylogenetic analysis detected a variety of enzymes with genus-specific multiplication, including both common enzymes and others that appear to be specific to pyrethrin biosynthesis. Together, these data identify possible novel components of the pyrethrin biosynthesis pathway and provide new insights into the unique genomic features of T. cinerariifolium."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Pyrethrum (Tanacetum cinerariifolium), which is a perennial Asteraceae plant with white daisy-like flowers, is the original source of mosquito coils and is known for the biosynthesis of the pyrethrin class of natural insecticides. However, the molecular basis of the production of pyrethrins by T. cinerariifolium has yet to be fully elucidated. Here, we present the 7.1-Gb draft genome of T. cinerariifolium, consisting of 2,016,451 scaffolds and 60,080 genes predicted with high confidence. Notably, analyses of transposable elements (TEs) indicated that TEs occupy 33.84% of the genome sequence. Furthermore, TEs of the sire and oryco clades were found to be enriched in the T. cinerariifolium-specific evolutionary lineage, occupying a total of 13% of the genome sequence, a proportion approximately 8-fold higher than that in other plants. InterProScan analysis demonstrated that biodefense-related toxic proteins (e.g., ribosome inactivating proteins), signal transduction-related proteins (e.g., histidine kinases), and metabolic enzymes (e.g., lipoxygenases, acyl-CoA dehydrogenases/oxygenases, and P450s) are also highly enriched in the T. cinerariifolium genome. Molecular phylogenetic analysis detected a variety of enzymes with genus-specific multiplication, including both common enzymes and others that appear to be specific to pyrethrin biosynthesis. Together, these data identify possible novel components of the pyrethrin biosynthesis pathway and provide new insights into the unique genomic features of T. cinerariifolium."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Pyrethrum (<i>Tanacetum cinerariifolium</i>), which is a perennial Asteraceae plant with white daisy-like flowers, is the original source of mosquito coils and is known for the biosynthesis of the pyrethrin class of natural insecticides. However, the molecular basis of the production of pyrethrins by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> has yet to be fully elucidated. Here, we present the 7.1-Gb draft genome of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, consisting of 2,016,451 scaffolds and 60,080 genes predicted with high confidence. Notably, analyses of transposable elements (TEs) indicated that TEs occupy 33.84% of the genome sequence. Furthermore, TEs of the <i>sire</i> and <i>oryco</i> clades were found to be enriched in the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific evolutionary lineage, occupying a total of 13% of the genome sequence, a proportion approximately 8-fold higher than that in other plants. InterProScan analysis demonstrated that biodefense-related toxic proteins (e.g., ribosome inactivating proteins), signal transduction-related proteins (e.g., histidine kinases), and metabolic enzymes (e.g., lipoxygenases, acyl-CoA dehydrogenases/oxygenases, and P450s) are also highly enriched in the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome. Molecular phylogenetic analysis detected a variety of enzymes with genus-specific multiplication, including both common enzymes and others that appear to be specific to pyrethrin biosynthesis. Together, these data identify possible novel components of the pyrethrin biosynthesis pathway and provide new insights into the unique genomic features of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Insect-borne infection is one of the most familiar and prevalent diseases worldwide. To date, a wide variety of insecticides and insect repellents have been developed to prevent insect-borne infection. Mosquito coils, which were first commercialized by Dainihon Jochugiku Co., Ltd., in 1902, are the origin of potent insecticides and insect repellents that are harmless to humans. The natural source of the original mosquito coil was the flower head of pyrethrum (<i>Tanacetum cinerariifolium</i>), a perennial Asteraceae plant with a white daisy-like flower (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1A</a>). The major insecticidal compounds are <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific specialized metabolites designated pyrethrins. These phytochemicals are biosynthesized by esterification of acid moieties (chrysanthemic acid and pyrethric acid) and alcohol moieties (pyrethrolone, jasmolone, and cinerolone)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kikuta, Y. et al. Identification and characterization of a GDSL lipase-like protein that catalyzes the ester-forming reaction for pyrethrin biosynthesis in Tanacetum cinerariifolium- a new target for plant protection. Plant J 71, 183–193, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.04980.x&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13285e416">1</a></sup>, yielding a total of six compounds, pyrethrin I and II, jasmolin I and II, and cinerin I and II, by combination of the acid and alcohol moieties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kikuta, Y. et al. Identification and characterization of a GDSL lipase-like protein that catalyzes the ester-forming reaction for pyrethrin biosynthesis in Tanacetum cinerariifolium- a new target for plant protection. Plant J 71, 183–193, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.04980.x&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13285e420">1</a></sup> in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>. Pyrethrins exhibit highly selective neurotoxicity to insects and are degraded rapidly in the presence of sunlight and oxygen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Zito, S. W., Zieg, R. G. &amp; Staba, E. J. Distribution of pyrethrins in oil glands and leaf tissue of Chrysanthemum cinerariaefolium. Planta Med 47, 205–207, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1055/s-2007-969986&#xA;&#xA; (1983)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13285e428">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="DeMicco, A., Cooper, K. R., Richardson, J. R. &amp; White, L. A. Developmental neurotoxicity of pyrethroid insecticides in zebrafish embryos. Toxicol Sci 113, 177–186, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/toxsci/kfp258&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13285e431">3</a></sup>. Characterization of pyrethrins led to the development of pyrethroids, structurally related compounds that are easily synthesized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Casida, J. E. Pyrethrum flowers and pyrethroid insecticides. Environ Health Perspect 34, 189–202, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1289/ehp.8034189&#xA;&#xA; (1980)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d13285e435">4</a></sup>. Both pyrethrins and pyrethroids have been extensively employed as common insecticides and insect repellents that have provided protection against insect-borne diseases such as malaria and dengue fever in many countries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Kulkarni, M. A. et al. Efficacy of pyrethroid-treated nets against malaria vectors and nuisance-biting mosquitoes in Tanzania in areas with long-term insecticide-treated net use. Trop Med Int Health 12, 1061–1073, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-3156.2007.01883.x&#xA;&#xA; (2007)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d13285e439">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Esu, E., Lenhart, A., Smith, L. &amp; Horstick, O. Effectiveness of peridomestic space spraying with insecticide on dengue transmission; systematic review. Trop Med Int Health 15, 619–631, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-3156.2010.02489.x&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d13285e442">6</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1102" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Flower of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> (<b>A</b>) and biosynthetic pathway of pyrethrins (<b>B</b>). Proposed biosynthetic pathway of pyrethrins. Chrysanthemic acid and pyrethric acid as acid moieties are synthesized from dimethylallyl diphosphates by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase (TcCDS), <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> alcohol dehydrogenase 2 (TcADH2), <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> aldehyde dehydrogenase 1 (TcALDH1), <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> chrysanthemol 10-hydroxylase (TcCHH), and <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> 10-carboxychrysanthemic acid 10-methyltransferase (TcCCMT). Pyrethrolone, jasmolone, and cinerolone as alcohol moieties are synthesized from linolenic acid by enzymes related to oxylipin pathway, including <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> jasmone hydroxylase (TcJMH), and unknown enzyme(s). Finally, chrysanthemoyl-CoA (the CoA-activated version of chrysanthemic acid) and pyrethrolone are esterified by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> GDSL (Gly-Asp-Ser-Leu motif) lipase (TcGLIP) to produce pyrethrin I. Compounds and enzymes are indicated by red and blue text, respectively. Unidentified enzymes are indicated as question marks.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To date, some pyrethrin biosynthesis-related enzymes have been identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kikuta, Y. et al. Identification and characterization of a GDSL lipase-like protein that catalyzes the ester-forming reaction for pyrethrin biosynthesis in Tanacetum cinerariifolium- a new target for plant protection. Plant J 71, 183–193, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.04980.x&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13285e488">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ramirez, A. M., Yang, T., Bouwmeester, H. J. &amp; Jongsma, M. A. A trichome-specific linoleate lipoxygenase expressed during pyrethrin biosynthesis in pyrethrum. Lipids 48, 1005–1015, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s11745-013-3815-1&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d13285e491">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, W., Zhou, F. &amp; Pichersky, E. Jasmone Hydroxylase, a Key Enzyme in the Synthesis of the Alcohol Moiety of Pyrethrin Insecticides. Plant Physiol 177, 1498–1509, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.18.00748&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13285e491_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Rivera, S. B. et al. Chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase: isolation of the gene and characterization of the recombinant non-head-to-tail monoterpene synthase from Chrysanthemum cinerariaefolium. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 98, 4373–4378, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.071543598&#xA;&#xA; (2001)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d13285e491_2">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xu, H. et al. Coexpression Analysis Identifies Two Oxidoreductases Involved in the Biosynthesis of the Monoterpene Acid Moiety of Natural Pyrethrin Insecticides in Tanacetum cinerariifolium. Plant Physiol 176, 524–537, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.17.01330&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d13285e491_3">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Xu, H. et al. Pyrethric acid of natural pyrethrin insecticide: complete pathway elucidation and reconstitution in Nicotiana benthamiana. New Phytol, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/nph.15821&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d13285e494">11</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>). Regarding the generation of alcohol moieties of pyrethrins, linolenic acid is converted to 13-hydroperoxylinolenic acid by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> 13-lipoxygenase (TcLOX1) and subsequently converted to jasmone by allene oxide cyclase (AOC), allene oxide synthase (AOS), 3-oxo-2-(2-pentenyl)-cyclopentane-1-octanoic acid reductase 3 (OPR3), and additional unknown enzymes. Jasmone is converted to jasmolone by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> jasmone hydroxylase (TcJMH). Although pyrethrolone and cinerolone are thought to be derived from jasmolone, the mechanisms and enzymes responsible for this conversion have yet to be elucidated. Regarding the synthesis of the acid moieties of pyrethrins, two molecules of dimethylallyl diphosphate are condensed to chrysanthemyl diphosphate by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase (TcCDS); the product subsequently is oxidized to chrysanthemic acid by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> alcohol dehydrogenase 2 (TcADH2) and <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> aldehyde dehydrogenase 1 (TcALDH1). The other acid moiety, pyrethric acid, is generated by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> chrysanthemol 10-hydroxylase (TcCHH) and <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> 10-carboxychrysanthemic acid 10-methyltransferase (TcCCMT). Finally, CoA-activated chrysanthemic acid (chrysanthemoyl-CoA) and pyrethrolone are esterified to pyrethrin I by <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> GDSL (Gly-Asp-Ser-Leu motif) lipase (TcGLIP). While TcGLIP has been isolated and identified as the enzyme catalyzing the esterification of pyrethrin I, the enzymes that catalyze the synthesis of pyrethrin II, jasmolin I, jasmolin II, cinerin I, and cinerin II have yet to be identified. In this context, elucidation of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome sequence is expected to provide crucial clues for clarification of the whole biosynthetic pathway of the pyrethrins and of the evolutionary processes. The genome sequence also is expected to facilitate the development of molecular tools for the generation of transgenic <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> and other systems for efficient and sustainable production of natural pyrethrins that can be difficult to chemically synthesize.</p><p>Genomes of various members of the Asteraceae family, including <i>Chrysanthemum seticuspe</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. De novo whole-genome assembly in Chrysanthemum seticuspe, a model species of Chrysanthemums, and its application to genetic and gene discovery analysis. DNA Res, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/dnares/dsy048&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13285e538">12</a></sup>, <i>Artemisia annua</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e544">13</a></sup> and <i>Helianthus annuus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature22380&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13285e550">14</a></sup>, have been reported over the past few years, but no pyrethrin-producing member of the Asteraceae family have been sequenced<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Katsuda, Y. Progress and future of pyrethroids. Top Curr Chem 314, 1–30, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/128_2011_252&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d13285e554">15</a></sup>. The genome of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> was estimated to be approximately 7.1 Gb by a flow cytometric method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bogunic, F., Siljak-Yakovlev, S., Muratovic, E. &amp; Ballian, D. Different karyotype patterns among allopatric Pinus nigra (Pinaceae) populations revealed by molecular cytogenetics. Plant Biol (Stuttg) 13, 194–200, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1438-8677.2010.00326.x&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d13285e562">16</a></sup>, indicating a genome that is more than twice the sizes of other Asteraceae genomes (e.g., 2.72 Gb for <i>C. seticuspe</i>, 1.74 Gb for <i>A. annua</i> and 3.6 Gb for <i>H. annuus</i>). Assembling such large genomes still remains challenging due to the presence of highly repetitive sequences, multiplied paralogous genes, and heterozygosity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Michael, T. P. &amp; VanBuren, R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr Opin Plant Biol 24, 71–81, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pbi.2015.02.002&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d13285e575">17</a></sup>. Here, we report a draft genome of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> derived by massively parallel sequencing using Hiseq4000 and X. The repetitive sequence content and multiplied pyrethrin-biosynthetic genes included in the assembled genome sequence also were analyzed.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Result"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Result</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequence assembly and annotation of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome</h3><p>Paired-end (PE) libraries and mate-pair (MP) libraries (with 3-, 5- and 8-kb insert sizes; MP-3 kb, MP-5 kb, and MP-8 kb, respectively) of the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome were generated and then sequenced using Hiseq X and Hiseq4000 instruments. The total bases of the sequence reads for PE, MP-3 kb, MP-5 kb, and MP-8 kb amounted to 1,497 Gb, 135 Gb, 166 Gb, and 95 Gb, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab1">1</a>). Subsequently, the output reads were subjected to contig assembly and scaffolding steps to estimate the genome sequence (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig2">2</a>). The 3,892,368 contigs with total length of 7.7 Gb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab2">2</a>) were constructed by assembling PE reads using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/2047-217X-1-18&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d13285e611">18</a></sup> and Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res 24, 1384–1395, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.170720.113&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d13285e616">19</a></sup> and merging the contigs using SSPACE-Longread<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-15-211&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13285e620">20</a></sup>. The assembled contigs then were scaffolded with PE and MP reads using SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btq683&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d13285e624">21</a></sup>, which concatenates contig sequences with pair information of the reads, and Gapfiller<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Nadalin, F., Vezzi, F. &amp; Policriti, A. GapFiller: a de novo assembly approach to fill the gap within paired reads. BMC Bioinformatics 13(Suppl 14), S8, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-13-S14-S8&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d13285e628">22</a></sup>, which fills the inter-contig unknown bases (‘N’s) in scaffolds with ‘A/T/G/C’ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig2">2</a>). The total length of the resultant scaffolds was 7.1 Gb, which corresponded with the flow cytometry-estimated genome size for <i>T. cinerariifolium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Bogunic, F., Siljak-Yakovlev, S., Muratovic, E. &amp; Ballian, D. Different karyotype patterns among allopatric Pinus nigra (Pinaceae) populations revealed by molecular cytogenetics. Plant Biol (Stuttg) 13, 194–200, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1438-8677.2010.00326.x&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d13285e638">16</a></sup>. The N50 value of the scaffolds was 14 Kb, and the maximum length of the scaffolds was 1.2 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab2">2</a>). Subsequently, the draft genome was subjected to Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btn013&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d13285e645">23</a></sup> with ‘intron hints’, which were generated with previous <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> transcriptomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Khan, S. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis reveals candidate genes for the biosynthesis of natural insecticide in Tanacetum cinerariifolium. BMC Genomics 18, 54, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s12864-016-3409-4&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d13285e652">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Guo, Y. et al. Identification and functional analysis of a cytochrome P450 gene CYP9AQ2 involved in deltamethrin detoxification from Locusta migratoria. Pestic Biochem Physiol 122, 1–7, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pestbp.2015.01.003&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d13285e655">25</a></sup>, resulting in the prediction of 935,992 putative genes.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of sequence reads.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="892" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Flowchart of genome assembly and gene prediction. The PE reads were subjected to contig assembly by a four-step process, including “pre-assembly” using PANDAseq, “contig assembly” using SOAPdenovo and Platanus, “clean-up” using BLASTN, and “merging with other assembly result” using SSPACE-longread. Then, the MP reads were subjected to scaffolding by a three-step process, including “read selection” using bowtie2, “scaffolding” using SSPACE-STANDARD, and “gapfilling” using GapFiller. These processes yielded the complete draft genome. The coding sequences in the draft genome then were annotated by a three-step process, including “gene prediction” using Augustus, “transposable elements (TEs) detection” using hmmpfam, and “protein superfamilies prediction” using InterProScan.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Statistics of genome assembly.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To evaluate the completeness of the genome, the draft genome sequences were subjected to analysis with BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol Biol Evol, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx319&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d13285e803">26</a></sup>, which counts complete (C), fragmented (F), and missing (M) conserved genes in genome sequences. The analysis with 1440 conserved core plant genes showed that 91.8% of conserved genes (82.3% as complete and 9.5% as fragmented) were present in the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome assembly (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab3">3</a>). Since these scores indicated quality as high as those of other plant genomes (with reported scores of 83.8% in <i>Cuscuta campestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Vogel, A. et al. Footprints of parasitism in the genome of the parasitic flowering plant Cuscuta campestris. Nat Commun 9, 2515, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/s41467-018-04344-z&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d13285e815">27</a></sup>, 77.5% in <i>Ruellia speciosa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Zhuang, Y. &amp; Tripp, E. A. The draft genome of Ruellia speciosa (Beautiful Wild Petunia: Acanthaceae). DNA Res 24, 179–192, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/dnares/dsw054&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d13285e822">28</a></sup>, 86.9% in <i>Erigeron breviscapus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yang, J. et al. Hybrid de novo genome assembly of the Chinese herbal fleabane Erigeron breviscapus. Gigascience 6, 1–7, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/gigascience/gix028&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d13285e828">29</a></sup>, and 89.0% in <i>Secale cereal</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Bauer, E. et al. Towards a whole-genome sequence for rye (Secale cereale L.). Plant J 89, 853–869, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/tpj.13436&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d13285e834">30</a></sup>), we used the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> draft genome for subsequent analysis.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Annotation statistics for draft genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To annotate the predicted genes, transposable elements (TEs) were first annotated using hmmpfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Eddy, S. R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d13285e906">31</a></sup> against the Gypsy database (GyDB)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Llorens, C. et al. The Gypsy Database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res 39, D70–74, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkq1061&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13285e910">32</a></sup>, revealing the presence of 525,098 TEs occupying 2,397 Mbp (33.84%) of the total genome. Further classification of TEs showed that 1,135 Mbp (16.02%), 907 Mbp (12.81%), 222 Mbp (3.13%), 88 Mbp (1.24%), 18 Mbp (0.25%), and 28 Mbp (0.39%) were occupied by Ty1/Copia, Ty3/Gypsy, Retroviridae, Caulimoviridae, Bel/Pao, and other TEs. Among Ty1/Copia and Ty3/Gypsy, the <i>sire</i> (Ty1/Copia, 11.16%), <i>athila</i> (Ty3/Gypsy, 5.76%), <i>del</i> (Ty3/Gypsy, 4.05%), and <i>oryco</i> (Ty1/Copia, 2.14%) clades were abundant (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab3">3</a>). The remaining 410,894 non-TE genes were subjected to InterProScan 5.6–48.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btu031&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13285e930">33</a></sup>, revealing 60,080 putative genes exhibiting known protein signatures. Thus, a high-quality 7.1-Gb <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> draft genome was assembled and shown to include a total of 525,098 TEs and 60,080 plausible genes based on 1,497 Gb of PE reads and 396 Gb of MP reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Inter-genus comparative analysis of TE classification</h3><p>We compared <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> TE clades with those of other species by predicting the TEs in the genomes of <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Nicotiana tabacum</i>, and <i>Oryza sativa</i>, revealing the presence in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> of two TE clades that were enriched by more than 2-fold compared to these other plant genomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab4">4</a>). In <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, members of the <i>sire-</i>clade TEs were found to be present at proportions 14.3-, 8.5-, and 8.1-fold higher than in <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>N. tabacum</i>, and <i>O. sativa</i>, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab4">4</a>). Similarly, members of the <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs were found to be present at proportions 2.2-, 4.6-, and 2.6-fold higher, respectively. These results indicated that <i>sire</i>- and <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs constitute a considerably larger proportion of the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome than is observed in these other plants. Furthermore, a comparison of the proportions of these TEs in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> to those of these TEs in other Asteraceae (<i>C. seticuspe</i>, <i>A. annua</i> and <i>H. annuus</i>) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab5">5</a>) demonstrated that the <i>sire</i>- and <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs are remarkably enriched in all of these Asteraceae genera. To assess whether <i>sire</i>- and <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs had multiplied in a common ancestor of the Asteraceae or independently in individual genera, we analyzed molecular phylogenetic trees of the reverse transcriptase (RT) domains encoded by the <i>sire</i> and <i>oryco</i> sequences (Supplemental Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">2</a>); specifically, we evaluated the number of co-clustered genes in single-genus clusters, which should reflect the number of genus-specific duplication events (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3</a>). If these TEs had multiplied in a common ancestor, most TEs would be expected to be positioned in orthologous clusters with bootstrap values greater than 50% (see Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3</a>, example of non-multiplied clusters). Otherwise, most TEs would be expected to be positioned in multiplied clusters (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3</a>). In fact, 98%, 92%, 93%, and 98% of <i>sire</i> TEs were separately multiplied in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3A</a>), <i>C. seticuspe</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3B</a>), <i>A. annua</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3C</a>), and <i>H. annuus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3D</a>), respectively, indicating that most of the <i>sire</i> TEs had multiplied in the individual genera. Likewise, 85%, 69%, 78%, and 89% of <i>oryco</i> TEs were multiplied in the respective organisms (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3E–H</a>), indicating that most of the <i>oryco</i> TEs also had multiplied in the individual genera. Moreover, 90% of <i>sire</i> TEs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3A</a>) and 49% of <i>oryco</i> TEs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3E</a>) clustered with eight or more multiplied genes. Collectively, these results suggest that <i>sire</i> and <i>oryco</i> TEs were multiplied in the individual lineages of the respective Asteraceae genera; in the case of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, TEs of these clades occupy approximately 13% of the genome.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Abundance score of TEs in other plants of the Asteraceae family.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab5" data-test="table-caption">Table 5 Abundance score of TEs in Asteraceae plants.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/tables/5"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="714" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Multiplication analysis of <i>sire</i>-clade (<b>A–D</b>) and <i>oryco</i>-clade (<b>E,F</b>) transposable elements (TEs). Based on molecular phylogenetic trees for <i>sire</i> TEs (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">1</a>) and <i>oryco</i> TEs (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">2</a>), the number of co-clustered genes in single-genus clusters, which reflects the number of genus-specific duplication events, were counted for each genus. The counted number of co-clustered genes for <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> (<b>A,E</b>), <i>C. seticuspe</i> (<b>B,F</b>), <i>A. annua</i> (<b>C,G</b>), and <i>H. annuus</i> (<b>D,H</b>) are shown in the form of pie charts.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also analyzed the distribution of TEs in genome scaffolds by counting the number of <i>sire</i> and <i>oryco</i> genes encoded in each 20-Kb region. As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig4">4</a>, uniform distributions of <i>sire</i> and <i>oryco</i> genes were observed using frequency plots for <i>sire</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig4">4A</a>) and <i>oryco</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig4">4B</a>) genes in each 20-Kb interval. Furthermore, no local distribution was detected by frequency plots for any of the TE clades (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig4">4C</a>). We next analyzed the number of TEs present within 20 Kb of another member of the respective TE clade. As shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig4">4D</a>, 82.35% and 92.65% of <i>sire</i> and <i>oryco</i> genes (respectively) were separated by more than 20 Kb from other TEs of the respective clade. Together, these data indicated that the TEs that multiplied in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, are scattered throughout the entire genome.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="674" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The distribution of transposable elements (TEs) in the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome. The distributions of TEs for <i>sire</i> (<b>A</b>), <i>oryco</i> (<b>B</b>) and all clade (<b>C</b>) were shown with the circles. The number of TEs coded in each scaffold regions within each 20 Kb region was plotted on the outer circle and %GC content in each 20 Kb was represented with heatmap on the inner circle. (<b>D</b>) The number of <i>sire</i>, <i>oryco</i> and all clade existing 20 Kb around respective TE was shown with histogram.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Functional annotation of the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genes and inter-genus comparative analysis</h3><p>Next, we investigated multiplication ratios of protein superfamilies in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> compared with those in other species. This analysis was performed using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btu031&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13285e1592">33</a></sup>. Specifically, the protein data sets of <i>C. seticuspe, A. annua, H. annuus, N. tabacum, O. sativa</i>, and <i>A. thaliana</i> were subjected to analysis using InterProScan and the multiplication odds scores were calculated for each combination of superfamily and species (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>). A positive value of the multiplication odds score indicates that a genus possesses a higher number of multiplied genes in a given superfamily than do other genera. As shown in Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>, the highest multiplication odds scores for <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> were observed for the biodefense-, signaling-, and metabolism-related superfamilies.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-6"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab6" data-test="table-caption">Table 6 Superfamilies with highest multiplication odds scores in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/tables/6"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>For biodefense-related superfamilies, “Ribosome-inactivating protein (RIP)” (IPR036041) and “Ricin B-like lectins” (IPR035992), both of which are categorized as type II RIPs, showed multiplication odds scores of 2.05 and 1.57 in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>). RIPs, including ricin, show high toxicity to a wide range of species, serving as biodefense molecules for the producing plant. For example, tobacco RIP, isolated and purified from <i>N. tabacum</i> leaves, has been reported to possess strong antibacterial activity against <i>Pseudomonas solanacearum</i>, <i>Erwinia amylovora</i>, and <i>Shigella asonei</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Sharma, N. et al. Isolation and characterization of an RIP (ribosome-inactivating protein)-like protein from tobacco with dual enzymatic activity. Plant Physiol 134, 171–181, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.103.030205&#xA;&#xA; (2004)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d13285e1955">34</a></sup>. Pokeweed antiviral protein, purified from the extracts of plant leaves of <i>Phytolacca americana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Obrig, T. G., Irvin, J. D. &amp; Hardesty, B. The effect of an antiviral peptide on the ribosomal reactions of the peptide elongation enzymes, EF-I and EF-II. Arch Biochem Biophys 155, 278–289 (1973)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d13285e1961">35</a></sup>, has been reported to enhance this plant’s systemic resistance to tobacco mosaic virus infection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhu, F. et al. Pokeweed antiviral protein (PAP) increases plant systemic resistance to Tobacco mosaic virus infection in Nicotiana benthamiana. European Journal of Plant Pathology 146, 541–549, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s10658-016-0938-2&#xA;&#xA; (2016)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d13285e1965">36</a></sup>. <i>Sambucus nigra</i> agglutinin I (SNA-I) has been shown to exhibit insecticidal potency against Hemiptera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Shahidi-Noghabi, S., Van Damme, E. J. &amp; Smagghe, G. Carbohydrate-binding activity of the type-2 ribosome-inactivating protein SNA-I from elderberry (Sambucus nigra) is a determining factor for its insecticidal activity. Phytochemistry 69, 2972–2978, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.phytochem.2008.09.012&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d13285e1972">37</a></sup>. Indeed, a BLASTP search of SNA-I sequence against <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> RIPs detected Tci_399175 with an E-value score of 2.99 × 10<sup>−112</sup> and 37.89% identity (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">3A</a>). Sequence alignment and BLASTP results revealed that Tci_399175 harbors the well conserved RIP domain and two RICIN domains containing Q-X-W motifs (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">3A</a>). In other work, RIP domains have been implicated in the cleavage of rRNA glycosidic bonds, while RICIN domains have been shown to be involved in internalization via binding of target cell glycans<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Walsh, M. J., Dodd, J. E. &amp; Hautbergue, G. M. Ribosome-inactivating proteins: potent poisons and molecular tools. Virulence 4, 774–784, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.4161/viru.26399&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d13285e1988">38</a></sup>. This sequence analysis suggested that the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-enriched RIPs include insecticides similar to SNA-I. These findings indicated that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> possesses not only low-molecular-weight insecticides such as sesquiterpene lactones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ramirez, A. M. et al. Biosynthesis of sesquiterpene lactones in pyrethrum (Tanacetum cinerariifolium). PLoS One 8, e65030, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0065030&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d13285e1999">39</a></sup> and pyrethrins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Zito, S. W., Zieg, R. G. &amp; Staba, E. J. Distribution of pyrethrins in oil glands and leaf tissue of Chrysanthemum cinerariaefolium. Planta Med 47, 205–207, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1055/s-2007-969986&#xA;&#xA; (1983)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2003">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="DeMicco, A., Cooper, K. R., Richardson, J. R. &amp; White, L. A. Developmental neurotoxicity of pyrethroid insecticides in zebrafish embryos. Toxicol Sci 113, 177–186, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/toxsci/kfp258&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2006">3</a></sup> but also a greater number of genes (compared with other plants) encoding native proteins with toxicity against insects.</p><p>For signaling-related superfamilies “Signal transduction histidine kinase, dimerization/phosphoacceptor domain” (IPR036097), “Rho GDP-dissociation inhibitor domain” (IPR024792), and “HECT, E3 ligase catalytic domain” (IPR035983) showed specific multiplication in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, with multiplication scores of 1.39, 1.32, and 1.14, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>). Some plant genera are known to possess histidine kinases (proteins that act as ethylene receptors) as well as non-ethylene-receptor histidine kinases (e.g., cytokinin receptors)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Sudre, D. et al. Iron-dependent modifications of the flower transcriptome, proteome, metabolome, and hormonal content in an Arabidopsis ferritin mutant. J Exp Bot 64, 2665–2688, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/jxb/ert112&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2019">40</a></sup>. A BLASTP search using <i>A. thaliana</i> ethylene-response 1 (ETR1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Chang, C., Kwok, S. F., Bleecker, A. B. &amp; Meyerowitz, E. M. Arabidopsis ethylene-response gene ETR1: similarity of product to two-component regulators. Science 262, 539–544, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.8211181&#xA;&#xA; (1993)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2026">41</a></sup>, an ethylene-activated kinase, as a query detected Tci_144982, a predicted protein that includes a G-X-G motif-containing HATPase_c domain, with an E-value score of 0 and 69.18% identity (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">3B</a>). This sequence analysis suggested that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-enriched histidine kinases include ethylene-receptor-type histidine kinases similar to ETR1. These <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific multiplications of signaling molecules suggested that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> has genus-specific enrichment of signal transduction and regulation proteins. While the molecular mechanisms of regulation of the accumulation and composition of pyrethrins are yet to be defined, the products of these enriched signaling-related genes are obvious candidates for regulators of pyrethrin biosynthesis.</p><p>The metabolism-related superfamily showed the strongest gene multiplication, with genes encoding “Enolase-like, C-terminal domain” (IPR036849), “Cytochrome c oxidase-like domain” (IPR036909) “Lipoxygenase, C-terminal domain” (IPR036226), “Acyl-CoA dehydrogenase/oxidase N-terminal domain” (IPR037069), and “Metal-dependent hydrolase, composite domain” (IPR011059) exhibiting multiplication scores of 1.28, 1.22, 1.16, 1.13, and 1.11, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>). Since lipoxygenases (e.g., TcLOX1) and acyl-CoA dehydrogenases/oxidases (enzymes catalyzing reactions related to the beta-oxidation of 12-oxo-cis-10,15-phytodienic acid) are known to be involved in the oxylipin pathway leading to the synthesis of pyrethrin alcohol moieties (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), these multiplication scores are compatible with the specific biosynthesis of pyrethrins and jasmonates in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>. Furthermore, “Cytochrome P450” (IPR036396) was detected with a multiplication score of 0.37, representing a total of more than 745 genes in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> and making the corresponding loci the most abundant class of genes in this plant. Indeed, TcJMH, a protein that is categorized as a P450, is known to be involved in the biosynthesis of jasmolone (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>); thus, the multiplication of P450s is consistent with a role of this protein class in the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific biosynthesis of jasmonates. The ferritin-like superfamily, members of which are responsible for iron transport, was found to have a multiplication score of 1.42. In <i>A. thaliana</i>, ferritins 1, 3, and 4 have been shown to regulate the expression of multiple genes encoding P450 enzymes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Briat, J. F. et al. New insights into ferritin synthesis and function highlight a link between iron homeostasis and oxidative stress in plants. Ann Bot 105, 811–822, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/aob/mcp128&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2068">42</a></sup>. A BLASTP search with <i>A. thaliana</i> ferritin 1 (AtFer-1) against <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> ferritin-like proteins detected Tci_154278 with an E-value of 5.64 × 10<sup>−115</sup> and 69% identity, including a conserved eukaryotic ferritin domain-containing iron ion channel, a ferroxidase diiron center, and a ferrihydrite nucleation center (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">3C</a>). In this regard, the multiplied genes encoding ferritin-like proteins in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> are expected to include proteins that modulate the expression of various P450s, presumably including one or more that affect TcJMH expression. This result is consistent with the duplication of genes encoding metalloenzymes such as metal-dependent hydrolases, cytochrome c, aconitases, lipoxygenases, and P450s (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>), and suggests that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> likely needs to import larger amounts (compared with other plants) of metals for use in various enzyme reactions and their regulation.</p><p>The lowest multiplication scores were found with “Expansin, cellulose-binding-like domain” (IPR036749), “Mitochondrial glycoprotein” (IPR036561), and “ArfGAP domain” (IPR038508), which exhibited multiplication scores of −0.53, −0.53, and −0.49, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab7">7</a>). The multiplication scores of these superfamilies in <i>O. sativa</i>, <i>C. seticuspe</i>, <i>A. annua</i>, and <i>H. annuus</i> were as low as the multiplication scores in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>), indicating that the depletion of the genome for members of this superfamily was not unique to <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> among the Asteraceae. In contrast, “Endochitinase-like”, a superfamily that plays a pivotal role in defense against fungal pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kasprzewska, A. Plant chitinases–regulation and function. Cell Mol Biol Lett 8, 809–824 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2122">43</a></sup>, exhibited a <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific low-copy status. The accumulation of RIPs (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>) and depletion of endochitinases suggested that the corresponding biodefense strategy has evolved specifically in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-7"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab7" data-test="table-caption">Table 7 Superfamilies with lowest multiplication odds scores in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/tables/7"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Phylogenetic analysis of pyrethrin-related enzymes</h3><p>One of the most characteristic features of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> is the presence of the pyrethrin biosynthetic pathway, components of which include TcLOX1 (<i>T. cinerariifolium</i> 13-lipoxygenase), TcJMH (<i>T. cinerariifolium</i> jasmone hydroxylase), TcCDS (<i>T. cinerariifolium</i> chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase), and TcGLIP (<i>T. cinerariifolium</i> GDSL (Gly-Asp-Ser-Leu motif) lipase). TcLOX1 and TcJMH are related to the lipoxygenase and P450 superfamilies, classes that were shown (by InterProScan analysis) to be enriched in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>. On the other hand, since TcCDS (unlike typical terpene synthases) has non-head-to-tail cyclase activity with isoprenoid units and TcGLIP (unlike typical GDSL lipases) has acyltransferase activity, there are no corresponding InterProScan superfamilies into which these enzymes can be categorized in terms of function. Therefore, we further analyzed the expansion of genes encoding TcLOX1, TcJMH, TcCDS, and TcGLIP using a BLAST search against the predicted proteomes of 13 different plant species available on public databases, and performed a molecular phylogenetic analysis using a maximum-likelihood tree with 500 bootstraps.</p><p>TcLOX1 is a member of a class of widely prevalent plant enzymes; TcLOX1 has been shown to be responsible for production of the alcohol moieties of pyrethrins via conversion of linolenic acid to 13-hydroperoxylinolenic acid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ramirez, A. M., Yang, T., Bouwmeester, H. J. &amp; Jongsma, M. A. A trichome-specific linoleate lipoxygenase expressed during pyrethrin biosynthesis in pyrethrum. Lipids 48, 1005–1015, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s11745-013-3815-1&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2319">7</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>). A BLASTP search using the TcLOX1 sequence as a query detected 134 proteins, including 9 <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins, 29 other Asteraceae proteins, and 96 non-Asteraceae proteins, with 42.03–57.58%, 42.31–95.26%, and 35.09–75.38% identity to TcLOX1, respectively. Molecular phylogenetic analysis of these proteins revealed that TcLOX1 formed an orthologous clade with <i>C. seticuspe, A. annua</i>, and <i>H. annuus</i> proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5A</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">4</a>, clade I). This clade was positioned proximally to another clade consisting only of Asteraceae proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5A</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">4</a>, clade II), indicating that these LOX families were duplicated in Asteraceae. In addition, these clades (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5A</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">4</a>, Clades I and II) belonged to a larger lipoxygenase clade (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5A</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">4</a>, clade III) that included <i>A. thaliana</i> LOX3 and 4, which have the same activity as TcLOX1. This LOX clade was consistent with the report that Asteraceae plants share the linolenic acid lipoxygenase activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Ramirez, A. M., Yang, T., Bouwmeester, H. J. &amp; Jongsma, M. A. A trichome-specific linoleate lipoxygenase expressed during pyrethrin biosynthesis in pyrethrum. Lipids 48, 1005–1015, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1007/s11745-013-3815-1&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2364">7</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Figure 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="672" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Enlarged view of molecular phylogenetic tree of pyrethrin-related enzymes. Clades I, II, and III for enzymes with similarity to <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> 13-lipoxygenase (TcLOX1) (<b>A</b>), <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> jasmone hydroxylase (TcJMH) (<b>B</b>), <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase (TcCDS) (<b>C</b>), and <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> GDSL lipase (TcGLIP) (<b>D</b>) are shown based on the results provided in Supplemental Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">4</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">7</a>, respectively. The nodes for pyrethrin-related enzymes are indicated in blue; <i>T. cineariifolium</i> nodes and edges are indicated in red; other Asteraceae nodes and edges are indicated in orange.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>TcJMH catalyzes the conversion of jasmone to jasmolone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, W., Zhou, F. &amp; Pichersky, E. Jasmone Hydroxylase, a Key Enzyme in the Synthesis of the Alcohol Moiety of Pyrethrin Insecticides. Plant Physiol 177, 1498–1509, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.18.00748&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2413">8</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), which has been reported only in the pyrethrin-synthesizing genus. A BLASTP search using the TcJMH sequence as a query detected 136 proteins, including 9 <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins, 30 other Asteraceae proteins, and 97 non-Asteraceae proteins with 34.22–81.53%, 37.67–85.01%, and 36.44–58.01% identity to TcJMH, respectively. Molecular phylogenetic analysis of these proteins revealed that TcJMH formed a subclade with one other <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> protein, two <i>A. annua</i> proteins, and one <i>C. seticuspe</i> protein (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5B</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">5</a>, Clade I) within a large clade consisting of two <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins and 18 Asteraceae proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5B</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">5</a>, Clade II). This inclusion within a large clade is in contrast with the fact that the conversion of jasmone to jasmolone has been reported only in the <i>Tanacetum</i> genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Li, W., Zhou, F. &amp; Pichersky, E. Jasmone Hydroxylase, a Key Enzyme in the Synthesis of the Alcohol Moiety of Pyrethrin Insecticides. Plant Physiol 177, 1498–1509, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1104/pp.18.00748&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2452">8</a></sup>. These results suggested that such non-<i>Tanacetum</i> TcJMH-like protein genes are non-functional or are involved in a pathway for biosynthesis of phytochemicals other than those observed in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>.</p><p>TcCDS participates in the formation of terpenoid acid moieties by conversion of dimethylallyl diphosphate to chrysanthemyl diphosphate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Rivera, S. B. et al. Chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase: isolation of the gene and characterization of the recombinant non-head-to-tail monoterpene synthase from Chrysanthemum cinerariaefolium.
