Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 98, 4373–4378, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.071543598&#xA;&#xA; (2001)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2465">9</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), which also has been reported to be a genus-specific pyrethrin-synthesizing reaction. A BLASTP search using the TcCDS sequence as a query detected 122 proteins, including 9 <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins, 28 other Asteraceae proteins, and 85 non-Asteraceae proteins with 61.27–88.54%, 36.36–93.52%, and 20.21–66.57% identity to TcCDS, respectively. Molecular phylogenetic analysis of these proteins revealed that TcCDS formed a subclade with three <i>A. annua</i> proteins and three other <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5C</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">6</a>, Clade I), within a large cluster consisting of seven <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins and 17 Asteraceae proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5C</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">6</a>, Clade II). The phylogenetic tree for TcCDS-like proteins did not include explicit orthologous clades; this result was consistent with a study demonstrating that the production of genus-specific monoterpenes, including chrysanthemyl diphosphate, has diverged in individual Asteraceae genera<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Liu, P. L., Wan, J. N., Guo, Y. P., Ge, S. &amp; Rao, G. Y. Adaptive evolution of the chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase gene involved in irregular monoterpene metabolism. BMC Evol Biol 12, 214, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2148-12-214&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2497">44</a></sup>. Previously, <i>A. annua</i> was shown to have a large number of terpene-related loci in its genome; the products of these genes are responsible for the biosynthesis of various genus-specific terpenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2505">13</a></sup>. These findings support the view that these terpene-biosynthesis genes also have multiplied and diverged in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, as reported for those of <i>A. annua</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2514">13</a></sup>.</p><p>TcGLIP synthesizes pyrethrin I by esterification of chrysanthemic acid moieties and pyrethrolone moieties in reactions specific to the pyrethrin-synthesizing genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kikuta, Y. et al. Identification and characterization of a GDSL lipase-like protein that catalyzes the ester-forming reaction for pyrethrin biosynthesis in Tanacetum cinerariifolium- a new target for plant protection. Plant J 71, 183–193, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.04980.x&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2522">1</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>). A BLASTP search using the TcGLIP sequence as a query detected 135 sequences, including 9 <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins, 30 other Asteraceae proteins, and 96 non-Asteraceae proteins with 48.73–81.78%, 31.87–79.35%, and 30.38–53.35% identity to TcGLIP, respectively. Molecular phylogenetic tree analysis of these proteins revealed that TcGLIP formed a subclade with one <i>A. annua</i> protein, two <i>C. seticuspe</i> proteins, and five other <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5D</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">7</a>, Clade I), within a large cluster consisting of nine <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> proteins and 12 Asteraceae proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5D</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">7</a>, Clade II). This phylogenetic tree demonstrated that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> has massively multiplied TcGLIP-related proteins.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Distribution analysis of pyrethrin-related enzymes</h3><p>Recent studies have shown that genes encoding components of a particular metabolic pathway, especially those responsible for specialized plant metabolites, are genomically co-localized and co-regulated as Metabolic Gene Clusters (MGCs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Field, B. &amp; Osbourn, A. E. Metabolic diversification–independent assembly of operon-like gene clusters in different plants. Science 320, 543–547, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.1154990&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2570">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Topfer, N., Fuchs, L. M. &amp; Aharoni, A. The PhytoClust tool for metabolic gene clusters discovery in plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 45, 7049–7063, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkx404&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2573">46</a></sup>. To find genes co-localized with pyrethrin-related MGCs, we initially subjected the genome sequences to PhytoClust<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Topfer, N., Fuchs, L. M. &amp; Aharoni, A. The PhytoClust tool for metabolic gene clusters discovery in plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 45, 7049–7063, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkx404&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2577">46</a></sup> with default settings for cluster range and flanking region. However, no MGCs that included loci encoding pyrethrin biosynthesis-related enzymes were detected in the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome. To permit further analysis, the distribution of genes within the scaffolds that included loci encoding TcLOX1, TcJMH, TcCDS and TcGLIP were analyzed using the GenomeJack software program. The TcLOX1-encoding gene was located on scaffold sc00017724 and showed co-localization with one gene encoding a hypothetical protein (Tci_094327) and four TE genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig6">6A</a>). A BLASTP search using the Tci_094327 sequence as a query detected the <i>Artemisia annua</i> squamosa promoter-binding (SPB) transcription factor (PWA57483.1) with an E-value of 2 × 10<sup>−35</sup> and 52.35% identity (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">8A</a>). Since the <i>A. annua</i> SPB transcription factor regulates the expression of proteins responsible for artemisinin B biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Lv, Z. et al. The SPB-Box Transcription Factor AaSPL2 Positively Regulates Artemisinin Biosynthesis in Artemisia annua L. Front Plant Sci 10, 409, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2019.00409&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2599">47</a></sup>, Tci_094327 is a candidate regulator of the oxylipin pathway. The TcJMH-encoding gene was positioned on scaffold sc00012411 and showed co-localization with two genes encoding putative transporters (Tci_072426 and Tci_072429) and three TE genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig6">6B</a>). The TcCDS-encoding gene was positioned on scaffold sc00057709 and exhibited co-localization with the gene encoding Tci_214196 (a protein that lacks apparent homologs by BLASTP) and one TE gene (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig6">6C</a>). These results indicated that genes encoding other pyrethrin-associated enzymes were not distributed proximal to the loci encoding either TcJMH or TcCDS. In contrast, the gene encoding TcGLIP was positioned on scaffold sc00006304 and co-localized with loci encoding two GDSL family lipases (Tci_043407 and Tci_043410), one glutathione S-transferase (Tci_043405), one hypothetical protein (Tci_043408), and two TE genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig6">6D</a>). Tci_043407 and Tci_043410 showed 50.53% and 41.10% identity to TcGLIP (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">8B</a>), respectively, suggesting that these genes had been generated by gene duplication and might encode enzymes with activities similar to that of TcGLIP. Thus, our results collectively indicated that a gene encoding the SPB transcription factor was proximal to the locus encoding TcLOX, and that genes encoding two GDSL lipases lie proximal to the locus encoding TcGLIP, suggesting that these novel genes may be co-regulated with those encoding pyrethrin-related enzymes known to be involved in pyrethrin biosynthesis.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Figure 6"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig6_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41598-019-54815-6/MediaObjects/41598_2019_54815_Fig6_HTML.png" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="485" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>Scaffolds including loci encoding pyrethrin-related enzymes. The predicted genes on the scaffolds containing genes encoding <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> 13-lipoxygenase (TcLOX1) (<b>A</b>), <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> jasmone hydroxylase (TcJMH) (<b>B</b>), <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> chrysanthemyl diphosphate synthase (TcCDS) (<b>C</b>), and <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> GDSL lipase (TcGLIP) (<b>D</b>) were visualized using GenomeJack. %GC content, transposable element (TE) coding regions, and non-TE coding regions are shown as bar-graphs, blue arrows, and red arrows, respectively. Red and pink boxes on non-TE gene arrows represent exons and protein-coding regions, respectively. Blue and sky-blue boxes on TE gene arrows represent exons and protein-coding regions, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p><i>T. cinerariifolium</i> is characterized by species-specific biosynthesis of the pyrethrins, a class of potent natural insecticides that are non-harmful to humans. A wide variety of specialized metabolites, including the pyrethrins, has been found to play defensive roles against several threats such as pests, pathogens, and herbivores<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Zito, S. W., Zieg, R. G. &amp; Staba, E. J. Distribution of pyrethrins in oil glands and leaf tissue of Chrysanthemum cinerariaefolium. Planta Med 47, 205–207, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1055/s-2007-969986&#xA;&#xA; (1983)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2660">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="DeMicco, A., Cooper, K. R., Richardson, J. R. &amp; White, L. A. Developmental neurotoxicity of pyrethroid insecticides in zebrafish embryos. Toxicol Sci 113, 177–186, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/toxsci/kfp258&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2663">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2666">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Singer, A. C., Crowley, D. E. &amp; Thompson, I. P. Secondary plant metabolites in phytoremediation and biotransformation. Trends Biotechnol 21, 123–130, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/S0167-7799(02)00041-0&#xA;&#xA; (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2669">48</a></sup>. Furthermore, the markedly larger genome size (7.1 Gb) of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, compared with those of other plants of the Asteraceae genera (2.72 Gb for <i>C. seticuspe</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. De novo whole-genome assembly in Chrysanthemum seticuspe, a model species of Chrysanthemums, and its application to genetic and gene discovery analysis. DNA Res, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/dnares/dsy048&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2678">12</a></sup>, 1.74 Gb for <i>A. annua</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2684">13</a></sup>, and 3.6 Gb for <i>H. annuus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature22380&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2691">14</a></sup>), suggests the occurrence of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific evolutionary processes. To address these issues, the present study explored the draft genome sequence of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>. In general, the assembly of large-sized genomes, such as that of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, is frequently hindered or precluded by numerous sequence errors due to inappropriate sequencing methods and the presence of repetitive sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Michael, T. P. &amp; VanBuren, R. Progress, challenges and the future of crop genomes. Curr Opin Plant Biol 24, 71–81, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pbi.2015.02.002&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2704">17</a></sup>. Therefore, we sequenced the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome to high depth, including 211-fold coverage by PE reads (to provide error correction) and 56-fold coverage by MP reads (to permit the assembly of repetitive sequences) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab1">1</a>). Furthermore, PANDAseq (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig2">2</a>) also has been shown to be effective for large-genome assembly, since this program includes Illumina-optimized error correction and longer-read generation using overlapping paired-end information<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Masella, A. P., Bartram, A. K., Truszkowski, J. M., Brown, D. G. &amp; Neufeld, J. D. PANDAseq: paired-end assembler for illumina sequences. BMC Bioinformatics 13, 31, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-13-31&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2718">49</a></sup>. The assembly strategy with high-depth reads used in the present paper eventually elucidated a 7.1-Gb genome sequence with a N50 of 14 Kb and 91.8% completeness as judged by BUSCO analysis (Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab2">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab3">3</a>). The resultant genome sequence revealed that TEs and multiple superfamily genes are enriched in the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome.</p><p>The <i>sire</i>- and <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs were shown to have accumulated after the divergence of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> from other Asteraceae (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig3">3</a>, and Supplemental Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">2</a>), with TEs of these clades occupying 11.16 and 2.14% of the genome sequence, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab4">4</a>). This accumulation of the <i>sire</i>- and <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs contributes to the large size of the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome. Furthermore, TEs have been suggested to be involved in the multiplication of plant disease resistance-related genes in <i>Capsicum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Kim, S. et al. New reference genome sequences of hot pepper reveal the massive evolution of plant disease-resistance genes by retroduplication. Genome Biol 18, 210, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s13059-017-1341-9&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2769">50</a></sup>. In this context, the accumulation of the <i>sire</i>- and <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> also may contribute to the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific enrichment of signaling-, biodefense-, and biosynthesis-related enzymes, as detected by InterProScan analyses.</p><p>The <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-enriched signaling-related superfamily genes included those encoding histidine kinases (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>), a group shown (using a BLASTP search and sequence alignment; Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">3B</a>) to include ethylene-receptor type histidine kinases similar to <i>A. thaliana</i> ETR1. These results suggested that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-enriched histidine kinases include proteins involved in responses to air-borne compounds. Notably, <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> upregulates pyrethrin biosynthesis by emitting a specific combination of volatile organic compounds (VOCs) when the plant body has been wounded<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Kikuta, Y. et al. Specific regulation of pyrethrin biosynthesis in Chrysanthemum cinerariaefolium by a blend of volatiles emitted from artificially damaged conspecific plants. Plant Cell Physiol 52, 588–596, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/pcp/pcr017&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2807">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Ueda, H., Kikuta, Y. &amp; Matsuda, K. Plant communication: mediated by individual or blended VOCs? Plant Signal Behav 7, 222–226, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.4161/psb.18765&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2810">52</a></sup>, suggesting that the upregulation of components of the pyrethrin biosynthetic pathway by wound-induced VOCs may occur via histidine kinase-mediated signal induction. Moreover, genes encoding lipoxygenases, which are known to contribute to the biosynthesis of VOCs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Scala, A., Allmann, S., Mirabella, R., Haring, M. A. &amp; Schuurink, R. C. Green leaf volatiles: a plant’s multifunctional weapon against herbivores and pathogens. Int J Mol Sci 14, 17781–17811, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.3390/ijms140917781&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2814">53</a></sup>, also were shown to be enriched in a genus-specific fashion in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>). These findings provide crucial clues to the investigation of the VOC-dependent regulation of pyrethrin biosynthesis.</p><p>The <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-enriched biodefense-related superfamily genes included those encoding ribosome inactivating proteins (RIPs) (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>), which are toxic to herbivores<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Shahidi-Noghabi, S., Van Damme, E. J. &amp; Smagghe, G. Carbohydrate-binding activity of the type-2 ribosome-inactivating protein SNA-I from elderberry (Sambucus nigra) is a determining factor for its insecticidal activity. Phytochemistry 69, 2972–2978, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.phytochem.2008.09.012&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2833">37</a></sup>. Furthermore, a BLASTP search and sequence alignment also suggested that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-enriched RIPs include insecticidal proteins similar to SNA-I (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">3A</a>). These results indicated that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> has the potential to produce such toxic proteins as biodefense factors, in addition to the production of insecticidal phytochemical toxins such as the pyrethrins. In contrast, the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome contains only a single gene encoding a putative endochitinase (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab7">7</a>), a class of proteins known to be involved in defense against fungal pathogens<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kasprzewska, A. Plant chitinases–regulation and function. Cell Mol Biol Lett 8, 809–824 (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2853">43</a></sup>. This observation accords with the low resistance of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> against serious foliar diseases caused by fungi such as <i>Stagonosporopsis tanaceti</i> and <i>Didymella tanaceti</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Scott, J. B. et al. Mycoflora Associated With Pyrethrum Seed and the Integration of Seed Steam Treatment Into Foliar Disease Management Strategies. Plant Dis 101, 1874–1884, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1094/PDIS-03-17-0309-RE&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2866">54</a></sup>, leading to the need for care in preventing fungal pathogen infection during the cultivation of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>. Altogether, the present results support the view that <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> might have been adapted to the dry environment of its area of origin, the Balkans, where fungi are less numerous than in the areas of the plant’s current cultivation. Consequently, this plant is likely to have been endowed with multiple RIPs as potent defenses against herbivores in addition to the acquisition of pyrethrins during the evolutionary processes.</p><p><i>T. cinerariifolium</i> also was found to be enriched for genes encoding members of the lipoxygenase and P450 superfamilies (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>), which are involved in the pyrethrin biosynthetic pathway. The existence of unique genes related to pyrethrin biosynthesis is one of the most prominent features of the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome. Molecular phylogenetic analyses of the enzymes for pyrethrin biosynthesis demonstrated that paralogs of TcGLIP, which synthesizes pyrethrin I (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), clustered in a <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific clade (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5D</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">7</a>, clade II), while paralogs of TcJMH and TcCDS, which synthesize jasmolone and chrysanthemyl diphosphate, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), clustered in Asteraceae-specific clades (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5B,C</a> and Supplemental Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">6</a>, clade II). In contrast, TcLOX1-related enzymes, which are widely prevalent plant enzymes responsible for the synthesis of 13-hydroperoxylinolenic acid (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), clustered in a clade including non-Asteraceae proteins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5A</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">4</a>, clade III). In particular, genes encoding TcCDS-related enzymes, which are categorized as terpenoid synthases, were as multiplied and varied in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> as in <i>A. annua</i>, where this class of proteins is known to biosynthesize various genus-specific terpenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2929">13</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5C</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">6</a>). Consequently, these multiplied genes encoding terpenoid-biosynthetic enzymes of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> are consistent with <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>-specific terpenoid synthesis. In contrast, TcJMH, the key enzyme for synthesis of the alcohol moieties of the pyrethrins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), was shown to be highly conserved in other plants (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5B</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">5</a>), whereas the alcohol moieties of pyrethrins, including jasmolone, pyrethrolone, and cinerolone, have been identified only in members of the <i>Tanacetum</i> genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kikuta, Y. et al. Identification and characterization of a GDSL lipase-like protein that catalyzes the ester-forming reaction for pyrethrin biosynthesis in Tanacetum cinerariifolium- a new target for plant protection. Plant J 71, 183–193, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.04980.x&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2959">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zito, S. W., Zieg, R. G. &amp; Staba, E. J. Distribution of pyrethrins in oil glands and leaf tissue of Chrysanthemum cinerariaefolium. Planta Med 47, 205–207, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1055/s-2007-969986&#xA;&#xA; (1983)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2959_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="DeMicco, A., Cooper, K. R., Richardson, J. R. &amp; White, L. A. Developmental neurotoxicity of pyrethroid insecticides in zebrafish embryos. Toxicol Sci 113, 177–186, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/toxsci/kfp258&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2962">3</a></sup>. These results raise the question whether these alcohol moieties have not yet been identified in non-<i>Tanacetum</i> plants, or whether TcJMH-like proteins can act on other substrates.</p><p>InterProScan analysis revealed that the <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genome is enriched for genes encoding members of the ferritin-like superfamily, proteins that are responsible for iron transport. Furthermore, various iron enzymes, including members of the lipoxygenase and P450 superfamily, a class that includes enzymes of the pyrethrin-biosynthesis pathway (including TcLOX1, TcJMH, and a putative P450 for conversion of jasmolone to pyrethrolone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Rettie, A. E., Rettenmeier, A. W., Howald, W. N. &amp; Baillie, T. A. Cytochrome P-450–catalyzed formation of delta 4-VPA, a toxic metabolite of valproic acid. Science 235, 890–893 (1987)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2975">55</a></sup>) also were shown to be multiplied (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Tab6">6</a>). Indeed, AtFer-1 is reported to regulate the expression of P450 in <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Briat, J. F. et al. New insights into ferritin synthesis and function highlight a link between iron homeostasis and oxidative stress in plants. Ann Bot 105, 811–822, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/aob/mcp128&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d13285e2984">42</a></sup>, and <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> is enriched for genes encoding ferritins, included a locus encoding a ferritin similar to AtFer-1 (Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">3C</a>, Tci_154278). Taken together, these results support the view that the ferritin-like superfamily and pyrethrin-biosynthetic lipoxygenase and P450s might have co-evolved in this lineage, given these proteins’ complementary roles in the uptake and biochemical use of iron. Thus, further work will need to examine the functional correlation of these multiplied ferritin-like superfamily proteins with multiplied genus-specific pyrethrin-biosynthesis enzymes.</p><p>Molecular phylogenetic analysis of TcGLIP, which plays a critical role in the esterification reaction that serves as the final step in the biosynthesis of pyrethrin I (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>), revealed that genes encoding TcGLIP-related proteins have diverged in the genome of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig5">5D</a> and Supplemental Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#MOESM1">7</a>). These results suggest that TcGLIP (and potentially other related proteins) acquired a role in esterification of pyrethrins in the specific lineage of this genus. Previously, the catalyzing activity of recombinant TcGLIP for the esterification of pyrethric acid with pyrethrolone was found to be lower than that of pyrethric acid with jasmolone, pyrethric acid with cinerolone, chrysanthemic acid with pyrethrolone, chrysanthemic acid with jasmolone, and chrysanthemic acid with cinerolone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kikuta, Y. et al. Identification and characterization of a GDSL lipase-like protein that catalyzes the ester-forming reaction for pyrethrin biosynthesis in Tanacetum cinerariifolium- a new target for plant protection. Plant J 71, 183–193, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2012.04980.x&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3010">1</a></sup>. Combined with those previous findings, the results of the present study suggested that the newly detected TcGLIP-family proteins likely catalyze uncharacterized esterification reactions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>). Collectively, the present genomic and molecular phylogenetic analyses are expected to contribute to the verification of pathways leading to the biosynthesis of unidentified pyrethrins (e.g., the conversion of jasmolone to pyrethrolone) and the underlying regulatory mechanisms. Furthermore, the molecular data obtained in the present study are expected to facilitate the creation of transgenic and genome-edited <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, paving the way to metabolic engineering for efficient phytochemical production, including that of additional pyrethrins.</p><p>The information on the co-localization (within the genome) of loci encoding related metabolic activities also is important, given that genes encoding components of a particular metabolic pathway, especially for specialized plant metabolites, frequently form such Metabolic Gene Clusters (MGCs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Field, B. &amp; Osbourn, A. E. Metabolic diversification–independent assembly of operon-like gene clusters in different plants. Science 320, 543–547, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.1154990&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3024">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Topfer, N., Fuchs, L. M. &amp; Aharoni, A. The PhytoClust tool for metabolic gene clusters discovery in plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 45, 7049–7063, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkx404&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3027">46</a></sup>. Although no MGCs for the enzymes known to be involved in pyrethrin biosynthesis were detected, a gene encoding a squamosa promoter-binding (SPB) transcription factor-like protein was observed to be co-localized with the gene encoding TcLOX1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig6">6A</a>). The <i>A. annua</i> SPB transcription factor regulates genes encoding proteins involved in the biosynthesis of artemisinin B, an A<i>. annua</i>-specific terpenoid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Lv, Z. et al. The SPB-Box Transcription Factor AaSPL2 Positively Regulates Artemisinin Biosynthesis in Artemisia annua L. Front Plant Sci 10, 409, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2019.00409&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3040">47</a></sup>. Since TcLOX1 is not involved in synthesis of the terpenoid subunit of pyrethrin but rather in the synthesis of the alcohol moieties of pyrethrins (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1</a>), it will be interesting to see if this co-localized SPB transcription factor-encoding gene is involved in pyrethrin biosynthesis. Similarly, we infer that newly detected TcGLIP-family proteins likely catalyze uncharacterized esterification reactions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>). Genes encoding two GDSL lipases also were found to be co-localized with the locus encoding TcGLIP (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig6">6D</a>). These newly detected lipases are candidates for uncharacterized esterification reactions involved in the synthesis of cinerin I and II, jasmolin I and II, and/or pyrethrin II (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#Fig1">1B</a>).</p><p>In conclusion, we have elucidated the draft genome of <i>T. cinerariifolum</i>, the natural source of the original mosquito coils. InterProScan and molecular phylogenetic analyses revealed the <i>T. cinerariifolum</i>-specific multiplication of genes encoding toxic proteins, metalloproteins, ferritins, histidine kinases, and pyrethrin biosynthetic enzymes, which are consistent with the unique physiological, ecological, and phytochemical features of these compounds. The draft genome opens new avenues for exploration of unidentified enzymes in pyrethrin biosynthesis and elucidation of the evolutionary processes of <i>T. cinerariifolum</i>, and provides fundamental information for the development of metabolic engineering of further beneficial phytochemicals using transgenic or genome-edited <i>T. cinerariifolum</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Materials and Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Materials and Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Plant materials and genome sequencing</h3><p>Wild-type <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> plants were grown and harvested under wild conditions in land surrounding the Onomichi City Museum of Art in Nishitsuchidocho, Onomichi, Hiroshima Prefecture, Japan. Genomic DNA was extracted from leaves and stems using a DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (Qiagen) according to the manufacturer’s instructions. Short-insert PE and MP libraries with three different insert sizes (3, 5 and 8 kb) of the extracted DNA were constructed using a TruSeq DNA PCR-Free kit (Illumina) and a Nextera Mate-Pair Sample Prep Kit (Illumina), respectively. The PE and MP libraries then were subjected to 150 × 2 cycles and 100 × 2 cycles of paired-end sequencing, using HiSeq X and HiSeq 4000 Illumina instruments, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec11">De novo assembly of genome sequences</h3><p>The obtained read sequences were cleaned for assembly as described in our previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Natsume, S. et al. The Draft Genome of Hop (Humulus lupulus), an Essence for Brewing. Plant Cell Physiol 56, 428–441, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/pcp/pcu169&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3095">56</a></sup>. Adapters derived from the Truseq or Nextera mate-pair Sample Prep Kit, low-quality reads, and short reads (&lt;36 bp in length) were trimmed by using Trimmomatic version 0.36<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114–2120, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btu170&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3099">57</a></sup>.</p><p>Contig sequences were generated from the PE reads by a four-step process (“pre-assembly”, “SOAPdenovo assembly”, “clean-up”, and “merging with other assembly result”). First, the cleaned PE reads, including overlaps, were detected and pre-assembled by PANDAseq<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Masella, A. P., Bartram, A. K., Truszkowski, J. M., Brown, D. G. &amp; Neufeld, J. D. PANDAseq: paired-end assembler for illumina sequences. BMC Bioinformatics 13, 31, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-13-31&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3106">49</a></sup>. Second, these pre-assembled reads and remaining reads were subjected to SOAPdenovo v2.04-r240<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/2047-217X-1-18&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3110">18</a></sup> with multi k-mers from 80 to 127 to generate contig sequences. Third, the SOAPdenovo-generated contigs were cleaned by trimming the contigs showing more than 95% sequence identity with other contig sequences. Fourth, PE reads also were subjected to processing using the Platanus assembler 1.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res 24, 1384–1395, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/gr.170720.113&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3114">19</a></sup> with default parameters, and the generated contigs were merged with SOAPdenovo-constructed contigs using SSPACE-longread v1-1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Boetzer, M. &amp; Pirovano, W. SSPACE-LongRead: scaffolding bacterial draft genomes using long read sequence information. BMC Bioinformatics 15, 211, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-15-211&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3118">20</a></sup>, including setting minimum overlap length to 36 bp, minimum number of links to 1, and maximum link ratio to 0.5.</p><p>Scaffold sequences were generated by a three-step process (“read selection”, “scaffolding”, and “gapfilling”). First, PE and MP reads were mapped to contig sequences using bowtie version 2.3.4.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Langdon, W. B. Performance of genetic programming optimised Bowtie2 on genome comparison and analytic testing (GCAT) benchmarks. BioData Min 8, 1, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s13040-014-0034-0&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3125">58</a></sup> with the–local option; read pairs mapped discordantly were selected for the following steps. Second, the selected reads and contigs were subjected to scaffolding using SSPACE-STANDARD version 3.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btq683&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3129">21</a></sup> (BaseClear), including setting the minimum number of links to 3. Third, the positions for which the bases were unknown (e.g., ‘N’) in the scaffolds were filled with ‘A/T/G/C’ by GapFiller v1-10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Nadalin, F., Vezzi, F. &amp; Policriti, A. GapFiller: a de novo assembly approach to fill the gap within paired reads. BMC Bioinformatics 13(Suppl 14), S8, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-13-S14-S8&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3133">22</a></sup> (BaseClear) to complete the draft genome, setting. including setting the minimum number of overlaps to 30 and the number of reads to trim off to 50. Furthermore, the completeness of the draft genome was evaluated using BUSCO-v3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol Biol Evol, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msx319&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3137">26</a></sup> with the embryophyta_odb9 protein set.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>The <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> RNA-Seq data available on NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRR2062279 and SRR2064180<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Khan, S. et al. Comparative transcriptome analysis reveals candidate genes for the biosynthesis of natural insecticide in Tanacetum cinerariifolium. BMC Genomics 18, 54, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/s12864-016-3409-4&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3152">24</a></sup>, as well as SRR5985187-5985194<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Guo, Y. et al. Identification and functional analysis of a cytochrome P450 gene CYP9AQ2 involved in deltamethrin detoxification from Locusta migratoria. Pestic Biochem Physiol 122, 1–7, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pestbp.2015.01.003&#xA;&#xA; (2015)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3156">25</a></sup>) were mapped to the assembled genome to generate the ‘intron hints’ for gene prediction. Subsequently, the assembled genome and ‘intron hints’ were subjected to Augustus 3.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btn013&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3160">23</a></sup> trained with available <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> genes in GenBank. TEs in predicted genes were identified by hmmpfam in HMMER 2.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Eddy, S. R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3168">31</a></sup> against GyDB 2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Llorens, C. et al. The Gypsy Database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res 39, D70–74, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkq1061&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3172">32</a></sup> with an E-value cutoff of 1.0. To identify high-confidence genes, the non-TE genes with known protein signatures were detected using InterProScan 5.6–48.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btu031&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3176">33</a></sup> and annotated using Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674–3676, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/bti610&#xA;&#xA; (2005)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3180">59</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Comparative analysis of TE content <i>versus</i> that in other plants</h3><p>The TE content also was estimated for six other genome-elucidated plants, including <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res 40, D1202–1210, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkr1090&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3199">60</a></sup>), <i>N. tabacum</i> (Ntab-TN90<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Sierro, N. et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun 5, 3833, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms4833&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3206">61</a></sup>)<i>, O. sativa</i> (assembly Build 4.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Rice Annotation, P. et al. The Rice Annotation Project Database (RAP-DB): 2008 update. Nucleic Acids Res 36, D1028–1033, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkm978&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3214">62</a></sup>)<i>, H. annuus</i> (HA412HO_v1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature22380&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3221">14</a></sup>), <i>A. annua</i> (ASM311234v1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3228">13</a></sup>), and <i>C. seticuspe</i> (CSE_r1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. De novo whole-genome assembly in Chrysanthemum seticuspe, a model species of Chrysanthemums, and its application to genetic and gene discovery analysis. DNA Res, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/dnares/dsy048&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3236">12</a></sup>). As described for TE detection in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, the coding regions of these genomes were estimated using Augustus 3.3.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Stanke, M., Diekhans, M., Baertsch, R. &amp; Haussler, D. Using native and syntenically mapped cDNA alignments to improve de novo gene finding. Bioinformatics 24, 637–644, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btn013&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3243">23</a></sup> with the <i>Arabidopsis</i> model set and the default parameters, and the TEs in these predicted transcriptomes were extracted using hmmpfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Eddy, S. R. Profile hidden Markov models. Bioinformatics 14, 755–763 (1998)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3250">31</a></sup> against GyDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Llorens, C. et al. The Gypsy Database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res 39, D70–74, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkq1061&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3254">32</a></sup>. According to GyDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Llorens, C. et al. The Gypsy Database (GyDB) of mobile genetic elements: release 2.0. Nucleic Acids Res 39, D70–74, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkq1061&#xA;&#xA; (2011)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3259">32</a></sup> classifications, the percentage of genomic regions occupied by each clade of TE was calculated as an accumulation score.</p><p>Molecular phylogenetic trees for <i>sire</i>- and <i>oryco</i>-clade TEs also were estimated using the ORTHOSCOPE method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Inoue, J. &amp; Satoh, N. ORTHOSCOPE: an automatic web tool for phylogenetically inferring bilaterian orthogroups with user-selected taxa. Mol Biol Evol, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msy226&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3272">63</a></sup> as described in our previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Shiraishi, A. et al. Repertoires of G protein-coupled receptors for Ciona-specific neuropeptides. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 116, 7847–7856, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1816640116&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3276">64</a></sup>. The amino acid sequences of hmmpfam-extracted reverse-transcriptase domains encoded by the TEs in <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>, <i>H. annuus</i>, <i>A. annua</i>, and <i>C. seticuspe</i> were aligned with CLUSTAL W-mpi 0.13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, K. B. ClustalW-MPI: ClustalW analysis using distributed and parallel computing. Bioinformatics 19, 1585–1586, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btg192&#xA;&#xA; (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3293">65</a></sup>, and maximum-likelihood phylogenetic trees based on a JTT matrix-based model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Jones, D. T., Taylor, W. R. &amp; Thornton, J. M. The rapid generation of mutation data matrices from protein sequences. Comput Appl Biosci 8, 275–282 (1992)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3297">66</a></sup> with 100 bootstraps were created using Fast Tree 2.1.10 (JTT model, CAT approximation)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Price, M. N., Dehal, P. S. &amp; Arkin, A. P. FastTree 2–approximately maximum-likelihood trees for large alignments. PLoS One 5, e9490, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0009490&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3301">67</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Comparative analysis of protein superfamily content <i>versus</i> that in other plants</h3><p>We also detected protein signatures using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btu031&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3318">33</a></sup> analysis in six genome-elucidated plants, using methods as described above for the InterProScan analysis of <i>T. cinerariifolium</i>. Having determined the number of genes possessing each superfamily signature, the multiplication odds score for each combination of InterProScan-detected superfamily signature (Sig) and plant genus (Genus) were calculated as follows:</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$${\rm{Multiplication}}\,{\rm{odds}}\,{\rm{score}}\,(\mathrm{Genus},\mathrm{Sig})={\log }_{2}\frac{N(Genus,Sig)+PS}{\overline{N(Sig)}+PS}$$</span></div></div><p>where N (Genus, Sig) represents the number of the plant-genus genes with an InterProScan-detected superfamily signature and PS represents the pseudo-count constant, which was set to 5.00.</p><p>In further analysis for functional proteins, we subjected the detected proteins of the superfamily with the highest multiplication odds score in each category to a BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res 25, 3389–3402, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/25.17.3389&#xA;&#xA; (1997)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3396">68</a></sup> search (version 2.7.1). <i>S. nigra</i> agglutinin I (SNA-I, accession No. O22415.1), <i>A. thaliana</i> ethylene-response 1 (ETR1, accession No. AAA70047.1) and <i>A. thaliana</i> ferritin-1 (AtFer-1, accession No. CAA63932.1) were used as queries for ribosome-inactivating proteins, signal transduction histidine kinase proteins and ferritin-like proteins, respectively. The detected protein pairs (SNA-I and Tci_399175, ETR1 and Tci_144982, and AtFer-1 and Tci_154278) were subjected to sequence alignment using CLUSTAL W-MPI 0.13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, K. B. ClustalW-MPI: ClustalW analysis using distributed and parallel computing. Bioinformatics 19, 1585–1586, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btg192&#xA;&#xA; (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3409">65</a></sup>.</p><p>Molecular phylogenetic trees were further estimated for <i>T. cinerariifolium</i> pyrethrin biosynthetic enzymes as described in our previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Inoue, J. &amp; Satoh, N. ORTHOSCOPE: an automatic web tool for phylogenetically inferring bilaterian orthogroups with user-selected taxa. Mol Biol Evol, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msy226&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3419">63</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Shiraishi, A. et al. Repertoires of G protein-coupled receptors for Ciona-specific neuropeptides. Proc Natl Acad Sci USA 116, 7847–7856, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1073/pnas.1816640116&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3422">64</a></sup>. Briefly, TcLOX1-, TcJMH-, TcCDS-, and TcGLIP-related proteins were identified using BLASTP 2.7.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Res 25, 3389–3402, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/25.17.3389&#xA;&#xA; (1997)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3426">68</a></sup> to search the protein sequences of <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Lamesch, P. et al. The Arabidopsis Information Resource (TAIR): improved gene annotation and new tools. Nucleic Acids Res 40, D1202–1210, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkr1090&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3433">60</a></sup>), <i>N. tabacum</i> (Ntab-TN90<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Sierro, N. et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat Commun 5, 3833, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/ncomms4833&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3441">61</a></sup>)<i>, O. sativa</i> (assembly Build 4.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Rice Annotation, P. et al. The Rice Annotation Project Database (RAP-DB): 2008 update. Nucleic Acids Res 36, D1028–1033, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkm978&#xA;&#xA; (2008)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3448">62</a></sup>)<i>, H. annuus</i> (HA412HO_v1.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Badouin, H. et al. The sunflower genome provides insights into oil metabolism, flowering and Asterid evolution. Nature 546, 148–152, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature22380&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3455">14</a></sup>), <i>A. annua</i> (ASM311234v1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Shen, Q. et al. The Genome of Artemisia annua Provides Insight into the Evolution of Asteraceae Family and Artemisinin Biosynthesis. Mol Plant 11, 776–788, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.molp.2018.03.015&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3463">13</a></sup>), <i>C. seticuspe</i> (CSE_r1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. DNA Res, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/dnares/dsy048&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3470">12</a></sup>), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (Pop_tri_v3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Tuskan, G. A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596–1604, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1126/science.1128691&#xA;&#xA; (2006)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3477">69</a></sup>), <i>Medicago truncatula</i> (MedtrA17_4.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Tang, H. et al. An improved genome release (version Mt4.0) for the model legume Medicago truncatula. BMC Genomics 15, 312, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2164-15-312&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3485">70</a></sup>), <i>Glycine max</i> (Glycine_max_v2.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178–183, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature08670&#xA;&#xA; (2010)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3492">71</a></sup>), <i>Zea mays</i> (B73_RefGen_v4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Jiao, Y. et al. Improved maize reference genome with single-molecule technologies. Nature 546, 524–527, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature22971&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3499">72</a></sup>), <i>Gossypium raimondii</i> (Graimondii2_0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423–427, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1038/nature11798&#xA;&#xA; (2012)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3507">73</a></sup>), <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (SL3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Shearer, L. A. et al. Fluorescence in situ hybridization and optical mapping to correct scaffold arrangement in the tomato genome. G3 (Bethesda) 4, 1395–1405, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1534/g3.114.011197&#xA;&#xA; (2014)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3514">74</a></sup>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (12X)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Canaguier, A. et al. A new version of the grapevine reference genome assembly (12X.v2) and of its annotation (VCost.v3). Genom Data 14, 56–62, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1016/j.gdata.2017.09.002&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3521">75</a></sup>. The BLAST hit sequences were screened using an E-value cut-off of 10<sup>−3</sup>, and the top ten hits were used for the subsequent phylogenetic analyses. These sequences were aligned using CLUSTAL W-MPI 0.13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, K. B. ClustalW-MPI: ClustalW analysis using distributed and parallel computing. Bioinformatics 19, 1585–1586, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/bioinformatics/btg192&#xA;&#xA; (2003)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3528">65</a></sup>, and maximum-likelihood phylogenetic trees based on a JTT matrix-based model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Jones, D. T., Taylor, W. R. &amp; Thornton, J. M. The rapid generation of mutation data matrices from protein sequences. Comput Appl Biosci 8, 275–282 (1992)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3532">66</a></sup> with 500 bootstraps were created using MEGA software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Li, M., Knyaz, C. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA X: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis across Computing Platforms. Mol Biol Evol 35, 1547–1549, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msy096&#xA;&#xA; (2018)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3536">76</a></sup>.</p><p>Metabolic Gene Clusters (MGCs) were investigated with PhytoClust<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Topfer, N., Fuchs, L. M. &amp; Aharoni, A. The PhytoClust tool for metabolic gene clusters discovery in plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 45, 7049–7063, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkx404&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3543">46</a></sup>; this analysis required definition of variables corresponding to the span of chromosomal region in which genes are clustered (cluster range) and the length of region to be searched for additional marker enzymes (flanking region). We set both variables to 20 Kbp, in accordance with the values used in the previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Topfer, N., Fuchs, L. M. &amp; Aharoni, A. The PhytoClust tool for metabolic gene clusters discovery in plant genomes. Nucleic Acids Res 45, 7049–7063, &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkx404&#xA;&#xA; (2017)." href="/articles/s41598-019-54815-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d13285e3547">46</a></sup>. The genes included in the same scaffolds as those encoding pyrethrin-related enzymes (TcLOX1, TcJMH, TcCDS and TcGLIP) were visualized with the GenomeJack software program (Mitsubishi Space Software, Tokyo, Japan).</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The draft genome sequences and annotations, along with the raw reads for PE and MP, have been deposited in the DNA Data Bank of Japan (DDBJ) under the BioProject accession Code PRJDB8358.</p>
